## Chapter: - Chapter 14: Seismic Data Interpretation:




### Section: 14.1 Seismic Attributes:

Seismic data interpretation is a crucial aspect of geophysical imaging, as it allows us to understand the subsurface structure and properties of the Earth. In this section, we will discuss the various attributes of seismic data and how they can be interpreted to gain insights into the Earth's near-surface.

#### 14.1a Amplitude Attributes

Amplitude attributes are one of the most important aspects of seismic data interpretation. They provide information about the strength and intensity of seismic signals, which can be used to identify different types of materials and structures in the subsurface.

The amplitude of a seismic signal is directly related to the strength of the source that generated it. This means that stronger sources, such as large earthquakes, will produce larger amplitude signals. However, the amplitude of a signal can also be affected by other factors, such as the distance between the source and the receiver, the type of material the signal is passing through, and the presence of any obstacles in its path.

One of the most commonly used amplitude attributes is the amplitude of the signal itself. This can be measured in terms of its amplitude in the time domain, or its amplitude in the frequency domain. The amplitude in the time domain is a measure of the strength of the signal at a specific point in time, while the amplitude in the frequency domain is a measure of the strength of the signal at a specific frequency.

Another important amplitude attribute is the amplitude envelope, which is the overall amplitude of the signal over a period of time. This can be used to identify changes in the strength of the signal, which can be indicative of changes in the subsurface.

In addition to these attributes, there are also more advanced techniques for analyzing amplitude data, such as spectral analysis and time-frequency analysis. These techniques allow us to break down the amplitude data into its individual frequency components, and to analyze how the amplitude changes over time.

Overall, amplitude attributes are a crucial tool for interpreting seismic data and understanding the near-surface structure of the Earth. By analyzing the amplitude of seismic signals, we can gain valuable insights into the properties and composition of the subsurface. 





### Section: 14.1 Seismic Attributes:

Seismic data interpretation is a crucial aspect of geophysical imaging, as it allows us to understand the subsurface structure and properties of the Earth. In this section, we will discuss the various attributes of seismic data and how they can be interpreted to gain insights into the Earth's near-surface.

#### 14.1a Amplitude Attributes

Amplitude attributes are one of the most important aspects of seismic data interpretation. They provide information about the strength and intensity of seismic signals, which can be used to identify different types of materials and structures in the subsurface.

The amplitude of a seismic signal is directly related to the strength of the source that generated it. This means that stronger sources, such as large earthquakes, will produce larger amplitude signals. However, the amplitude of a signal can also be affected by other factors, such as the distance between the source and the receiver, the type of material the signal is passing through, and the presence of any obstacles in its path.

One of the most commonly used amplitude attributes is the amplitude of the signal itself. This can be measured in terms of its amplitude in the time domain, or its amplitude in the frequency domain. The amplitude in the time domain is a measure of the strength of the signal at a specific point in time, while the amplitude in the frequency domain is a measure of the strength of the signal at a specific frequency.

Another important amplitude attribute is the amplitude envelope, which is the overall amplitude of the signal over a period of time. This can be used to identify changes in the strength of the signal, which can be indicative of changes in the subsurface.

In addition to these attributes, there are also more advanced techniques for analyzing amplitude data, such as spectral analysis and time-frequency analysis. These techniques allow us to break down the amplitude data into its individual frequency components, providing a more detailed understanding of the subsurface.

#### 14.1b Frequency Attributes

Frequency attributes are another important aspect of seismic data interpretation. They provide information about the frequency content of seismic signals, which can be used to identify different types of materials and structures in the subsurface.

The frequency of a seismic signal is directly related to the speed of the source that generated it. This means that faster sources, such as small earthquakes, will produce higher frequency signals. However, the frequency of a signal can also be affected by other factors, such as the distance between the source and the receiver, the type of material the signal is passing through, and the presence of any obstacles in its path.

One of the most commonly used frequency attributes is the frequency of the signal itself. This can be measured in terms of its frequency in the time domain, or its frequency in the frequency domain. The frequency in the time domain is a measure of the speed of the signal at a specific point in time, while the frequency in the frequency domain is a measure of the speed of the signal at a specific frequency.

Another important frequency attribute is the frequency envelope, which is the overall frequency of the signal over a period of time. This can be used to identify changes in the speed of the signal, which can be indicative of changes in the subsurface.

In addition to these attributes, there are also more advanced techniques for analyzing frequency data, such as spectral analysis and time-frequency analysis. These techniques allow us to break down the frequency data into its individual components, providing a more detailed understanding of the subsurface.

#### 14.1c Polarity Attributes

Polarity attributes are a crucial aspect of seismic data interpretation. They provide information about the direction of energy flow in seismic signals, which can be used to identify different types of materials and structures in the subsurface.

The polarity of a seismic signal is determined by the direction of energy flow, which can be either positive or negative. Positive polarity indicates that the energy is moving towards the receiver, while negative polarity indicates that the energy is moving away from the receiver.

One of the most commonly used polarity attributes is the polarity of the signal itself. This can be measured in terms of its polarity in the time domain, or its polarity in the frequency domain. The polarity in the time domain is a measure of the direction of energy flow at a specific point in time, while the polarity in the frequency domain is a measure of the direction of energy flow at a specific frequency.

Another important polarity attribute is the polarity envelope, which is the overall polarity of the signal over a period of time. This can be used to identify changes in the direction of energy flow, which can be indicative of changes in the subsurface.

In addition to these attributes, there are also more advanced techniques for analyzing polarity data, such as spectral analysis and time-frequency analysis. These techniques allow us to break down the polarity data into its individual components, providing a more detailed understanding of the subsurface.





### Section: 14.1 Seismic Attributes:

Seismic data interpretation is a crucial aspect of geophysical imaging, as it allows us to understand the subsurface structure and properties of the Earth. In this section, we will discuss the various attributes of seismic data and how they can be interpreted to gain insights into the Earth's near-surface.

#### 14.1a Amplitude Attributes

Amplitude attributes are one of the most important aspects of seismic data interpretation. They provide information about the strength and intensity of seismic signals, which can be used to identify different types of materials and structures in the subsurface.

The amplitude of a seismic signal is directly related to the strength of the source that generated it. This means that stronger sources, such as large earthquakes, will produce larger amplitude signals. However, the amplitude of a signal can also be affected by other factors, such as the distance between the source and the receiver, the type of material the signal is passing through, and the presence of any obstacles in its path.

One of the most commonly used amplitude attributes is the amplitude of the signal itself. This can be measured in terms of its amplitude in the time domain, or its amplitude in the frequency domain. The amplitude in the time domain is a measure of the strength of the signal at a specific point in time, while the amplitude in the frequency domain is a measure of the strength of the signal at a specific frequency.

Another important amplitude attribute is the amplitude envelope, which is the overall amplitude of the signal over a period of time. This can be used to identify changes in the strength of the signal, which can be indicative of changes in the subsurface.

In addition to these attributes, there are also more advanced techniques for analyzing amplitude data, such as spectral analysis and time-frequency analysis. These techniques allow us to break down the amplitude data into its individual frequency components, providing a more detailed understanding of the subsurface.

#### 14.1b Frequency Attributes

Frequency attributes are another important aspect of seismic data interpretation. They provide information about the rate at which the amplitude of a signal changes over time. This can be useful in identifying different types of materials and structures in the subsurface.

The frequency of a seismic signal is directly related to the speed at which the source is moving. This means that faster moving sources, such as small earthquakes, will produce higher frequency signals. However, the frequency of a signal can also be affected by other factors, such as the type of material the signal is passing through and the presence of any obstacles in its path.

One of the most commonly used frequency attributes is the frequency of the signal itself. This can be measured in terms of its frequency in the time domain, or its frequency in the frequency domain. The frequency in the time domain is a measure of the rate at which the amplitude of the signal changes over time, while the frequency in the frequency domain is a measure of the rate at which the amplitude of the signal changes at a specific frequency.

Another important frequency attribute is the frequency envelope, which is the overall frequency of the signal over a period of time. This can be used to identify changes in the frequency of the signal, which can be indicative of changes in the subsurface.

In addition to these attributes, there are also more advanced techniques for analyzing frequency data, such as spectral analysis and time-frequency analysis. These techniques allow us to break down the frequency data into its individual components, providing a more detailed understanding of the subsurface.

#### 14.1c Textural Attributes

Textural attributes are a relatively new aspect of seismic data interpretation. They provide information about the texture of the subsurface, which can be useful in identifying different types of materials and structures.

The texture of a subsurface is determined by the arrangement and distribution of the particles that make up the material. This can be influenced by factors such as the type of material, the presence of fluids, and the presence of fractures or cracks.

One of the most commonly used textural attributes is the texture of the subsurface itself. This can be measured in terms of its texture in the time domain, or its texture in the frequency domain. The texture in the time domain is a measure of the arrangement and distribution of particles over time, while the texture in the frequency domain is a measure of the arrangement and distribution of particles at a specific frequency.

Another important textural attribute is the texture envelope, which is the overall texture of the subsurface over a period of time. This can be used to identify changes in the texture of the subsurface, which can be indicative of changes in the subsurface.

In addition to these attributes, there are also more advanced techniques for analyzing textural data, such as spectral analysis and time-frequency analysis. These techniques allow us to break down the textural data into its individual components, providing a more detailed understanding of the subsurface.

### Subsection: 14.1c Textural Attributes

Textural attributes are a relatively new aspect of seismic data interpretation. They provide information about the texture of the subsurface, which can be useful in identifying different types of materials and structures.

The texture of a subsurface is determined by the arrangement and distribution of the particles that make up the material. This can be influenced by factors such as the type of material, the presence of fluids, and the presence of fractures or cracks.

One of the most commonly used textural attributes is the texture of the subsurface itself. This can be measured in terms of its texture in the time domain, or its texture in the frequency domain. The texture in the time domain is a measure of the arrangement and distribution of particles over time, while the texture in the frequency domain is a measure of the arrangement and distribution of particles at a specific frequency.

Another important textural attribute is the texture envelope, which is the overall texture of the subsurface over a period of time. This can be used to identify changes in the texture of the subsurface, which can be indicative of changes in the subsurface.

In addition to these attributes, there are also more advanced techniques for analyzing textural data, such as spectral analysis and time-frequency analysis. These techniques allow us to break down the textural data into its individual components, providing a more detailed understanding of the subsurface.

## Solid representation schemes

Based on assumed mathematical properties, any scheme of representing solids is a method for capturing information about the class of semi-analytic subsets of Euclidean space. This means all representations are different ways of organizing the same geometric and topological data in the form of a data structure. All representation schemes are organized in terms of a finite number of operations on a set of primitives. Therefore, the modeling space of any particular representation is finite, and any single representation scheme may not completely suffice to represent all types of solids. For example, solids defined via combinations of regularized boolean operations cannot necessarily be represented as the sweep of a primitive moving according to a space trajectory, except in very simple cases. This forces modern geometric modeling systems to maintain several representation schemes of solids and also facilitate efficient conversion between representation schemes.

Below is a list of common techniques used to create or represent solid models. Modern modeling software may use a combination of these schemes to represent a solid.

### Primitive instancing

This scheme is based on notion of families of object, each member of a family distinguishable from the other by a few parameters. Each object family is represented by a set of primitives, which are geometric objects such as points, lines, and surfaces. The primitives are instanced, or copied, to create a family of objects. This scheme is useful for creating complex structures, such as buildings or landscapes, by combining simple primitives.

### Boundary representation

Boundary representation, or B-rep, is a common technique for representing solids. In this scheme, a solid is represented by its boundary, which is a set of faces, edges, and vertices. The faces are polygons, the edges are lines, and the vertices are points. This representation is useful for creating smooth, continuous surfaces, but it can be difficult to modify or edit the solid once it is created.

### Constructive solid geometry

Constructive solid geometry, or CSG, is a technique for representing solids by constructing them from simpler shapes. This is done by performing Boolean operations, such as union, intersection, and difference, on a set of primitive shapes. CSG is useful for creating complex solids, but it can be difficult to visualize and manipulate the resulting solid.

### Solid of revolution

A solid of revolution is a solid created by rotating a curve or surface around an axis. This technique is useful for creating cylindrical or spherical solids, but it can be limited in its ability to create more complex shapes.

### Swept surface

A swept surface is a solid created by moving a surface along a path. This technique is useful for creating solids with curved surfaces, such as a cylinder or a cone.

### Subdivision surface

A subdivision surface is a smooth surface created by subdividing a simpler surface into smaller, more detailed surfaces. This technique is useful for creating smooth, organic shapes, but it can be computationally intensive.

### Point cloud

A point cloud is a set of points in three-dimensional space. This representation is useful for capturing the surface of an object, but it can be difficult to interpret and manipulate.

### Mesh

A mesh is a set of connected polygons that form a surface. This representation is useful for creating complex surfaces, but it can be difficult to modify or edit the surface once it is created.

### NURBS

Non-uniform rational B-splines, or NURBS, are a mathematical representation of curves and surfaces. This representation is useful for creating smooth, continuous surfaces, but it can be difficult to visualize and manipulate.

### Polyhedron

A polyhedron is a solid created by joining a set of polygons along their edges. This representation is useful for creating simple solids, but it can be limited in its ability to create more complex shapes.

### Tessellation

A tessellation is a set of polygons that cover a surface without overlapping. This representation is useful for creating smooth, continuous surfaces, but it can be difficult to modify or edit the surface once it is created.

### Geometric attributes

Geometric attributes are properties of a solid that describe its shape and size. These attributes can include the number of vertices, edges, and faces, as well as the volume, surface area, and curvature of the solid. These attributes can be useful for identifying and classifying different types of solids.

### Topological attributes

Topological attributes are properties of a solid that describe its connectivity and relationships between its parts. These attributes can include the number of connected components, the number of holes, and the Euler characteristic of the solid. These attributes can be useful for identifying and classifying different types of solids.

### Material attributes

Material attributes are properties of a solid that describe its physical properties, such as its density, elasticity, and conductivity. These attributes can be useful for identifying and classifying different types of solids, as well as predicting their behavior under different conditions.

### Textural attributes

Textural attributes are properties of a solid that describe its surface texture, such as its roughness, smoothness, and pattern. These attributes can be useful for identifying and classifying different types of solids, as well as predicting their behavior under different conditions.

### Other attributes

Other attributes, such as color, transparency, and reflectivity, can also be used to describe and classify solids. These attributes can be useful for identifying and classifying different types of solids, as well as predicting their behavior under different conditions.

## Chapter: - Chapter 15: Seismic Inversion:




### Section: 15.1 Deterministic Inversion:

Deterministic inversion is a powerful technique used in geophysics to extract information about the subsurface from seismic data. It involves the use of mathematical models and algorithms to invert the seismic data and create a detailed image of the subsurface. This section will provide an introduction to deterministic inversion and discuss its applications in geophysics.

#### 15.1a Acoustic Inversion

Acoustic inversion is a type of deterministic inversion that is commonly used in geophysics. It involves the use of acoustic waves to image the subsurface. Acoustic waves are mechanical waves that travel through a medium, and they are commonly used in seismic exploration due to their ability to penetrate deep into the Earth's crust.

The process of acoustic inversion involves the use of mathematical models and algorithms to invert the seismic data and create a detailed image of the subsurface. This is done by analyzing the properties of the acoustic waves, such as their velocity and attenuation, as they travel through the subsurface. By inverting the data, geophysicists can determine the properties of the subsurface, such as its density and elasticity.

One of the key advantages of acoustic inversion is its ability to provide high-resolution images of the subsurface. This is due to the fact that acoustic waves are sensitive to small changes in the properties of the subsurface, making them ideal for imaging shallow structures. Additionally, acoustic inversion can be used to image both the near-surface and deep subsurface, making it a versatile technique for geophysical exploration.

However, there are also limitations to acoustic inversion. One of the main challenges is the presence of noise and interference in the seismic data. This can make it difficult to accurately interpret the data and create a detailed image of the subsurface. Additionally, acoustic inversion relies on assumptions about the properties of the subsurface, which may not always be accurate.

Despite these limitations, acoustic inversion remains a valuable tool in geophysical exploration. It is commonly used in conjunction with other techniques, such as electromagnetic inversion, to provide a more comprehensive understanding of the subsurface. In the next section, we will discuss another type of deterministic inversion - electromagnetic inversion.





### Section: 15.1b Elastic Inversion

Elastic inversion is another type of deterministic inversion that is commonly used in geophysics. It involves the use of elastic waves to image the subsurface. Elastic waves are mechanical waves that travel through a medium, and they are commonly used in seismic exploration due to their ability to penetrate deep into the Earth's crust.

The process of elastic inversion involves the use of mathematical models and algorithms to invert the seismic data and create a detailed image of the subsurface. This is done by analyzing the properties of the elastic waves, such as their velocity and attenuation, as they travel through the subsurface. By inverting the data, geophysicists can determine the properties of the subsurface, such as its density and elasticity.

One of the key advantages of elastic inversion is its ability to provide high-resolution images of the subsurface. This is due to the fact that elastic waves are sensitive to small changes in the properties of the subsurface, making them ideal for imaging both shallow and deep structures. Additionally, elastic inversion can be used to image both the near-surface and deep subsurface, making it a versatile technique for geophysical exploration.

However, there are also limitations to elastic inversion. One of the main challenges is the presence of noise and interference in the seismic data. This can make it difficult to accurately interpret the data and create a detailed image of the subsurface. Additionally, elastic inversion relies on assumptions about the properties of the subsurface, such as homogeneity and isotropy, which may not always be accurate.

### Subsection: 15.1b.1 Pre-Stack Seismic Resolution Inversion

Pre-stack inversion is often used when post-stack inversion fails to sufficiently differentiate geologic features with similar P-impedance signatures. Simultaneous inversion solves for S-impedance and density, in addition to P-impedance. While many geologic features can express similar P-impedance characteristics, few will share combined P-impedance and S-impedance traits (allowing improved separation and clarity). Often a feasibility study using the wells logs will indicate whether separation of the desired lithotype can be achieved with P-impedance alone or whether S-impedance is also required. This will dictate whether a pre- or post-stack inversion is needed.

Simultaneous Inversion (SI) is a pre-stack method that uses multiple offset or angle seismic sub-stacks and their associated wavelets as input; it generates P-impedance, S-impedance and density as outputs (although the density output resolution is rarely as high as the impedances). This helps improve discrimination between lithology, porosity and fluid effects. For each input partial stack, a unique wavelet is estimated. All models, partial stacks and wavelets are input to a single inversion algorithm — enabling inversion to effectively compensate for offset-dependent phase, bandwidth, tuning and NMO stretch effects.

The inversion algorithm works by first estimating angle-dependent P-wave reflectivities for the input-partial stacks. Next, these are used with the full Zoeppritz equations (or approximations, such as Aki–Richards, for some algorithms) to find band-limited elastic reflectivities. These are in turn merged with their low-frequency counterparts from the model and integrated to elastic properties. This approximate result is then improved in a final inversion for P-impedance, S-impedance and density, subject to various hard and soft constraints. One constraint can control the resolution of the inversion, with higher resolution resulting in a more detailed image of the subsurface.

### Subsection: 15.1b.2 Post-Stack Seismic Resolution Inversion

Post-stack inversion is another type of deterministic inversion that is commonly used in geophysics. It involves the use of post-stack data, which is data that has been processed and stacked together from multiple sources. This type of inversion is often used when pre-stack inversion is not feasible or when the data is already processed and stacked.

The process of post-stack inversion involves the use of mathematical models and algorithms to invert the post-stack data and create a detailed image of the subsurface. This is done by analyzing the properties of the post-stack data, such as its velocity and attenuation, as they travel through the subsurface. By inverting the data, geophysicists can determine the properties of the subsurface, such as its density and elasticity.

One of the key advantages of post-stack inversion is its ability to provide high-resolution images of the subsurface. This is due to the fact that post-stack data is already processed and stacked, making it easier to interpret and invert. Additionally, post-stack inversion can be used to image both the near-surface and deep subsurface, making it a versatile technique for geophysical exploration.

However, there are also limitations to post-stack inversion. One of the main challenges is the presence of noise and interference in the post-stack data. This can make it difficult to accurately interpret the data and create a detailed image of the subsurface. Additionally, post-stack inversion relies on assumptions about the properties of the subsurface, such as homogeneity and isotropy, which may not always be accurate.


## Chapter 1:5: Seismic Inversion:




### Section: 15.1c Joint Inversion

Joint inversion is a powerful technique used in geophysics to image the subsurface. It combines the principles of both elastic and acoustic inversion to provide a more comprehensive understanding of the subsurface properties. This technique is particularly useful in complex geological environments where traditional inversion methods may not be sufficient.

The process of joint inversion involves the use of both P-waves and S-waves to image the subsurface. P-waves, or primary waves, are compressional waves that travel through the subsurface, while S-waves, or shear waves, are shear waves that travel along the subsurface. By analyzing the properties of these waves, such as their velocity and attenuation, joint inversion can provide a more detailed image of the subsurface.

One of the key advantages of joint inversion is its ability to image both the near-surface and deep subsurface. This is due to the fact that P-waves and S-waves have different propagation characteristics, allowing for a more comprehensive understanding of the subsurface properties. Additionally, joint inversion can be used to image both homogeneous and heterogeneous subsurface structures, making it a versatile technique for geophysical exploration.

However, there are also limitations to joint inversion. One of the main challenges is the presence of noise and interference in the seismic data. This can make it difficult to accurately interpret the data and create a detailed image of the subsurface. Additionally, joint inversion relies on assumptions about the properties of the subsurface, such as homogeneity and isotropy, which may not always be accurate.

### Subsection: 15.1c.1 Pre-Stack Joint Inversion

Pre-stack joint inversion is a type of joint inversion that is commonly used in geophysics. It involves the use of pre-stack data, which is data collected before the seismic event has occurred. This type of inversion is particularly useful in complex geological environments where traditional post-stack inversion may not be sufficient.

The process of pre-stack joint inversion involves the use of both P-waves and S-waves to image the subsurface. By analyzing the properties of these waves, such as their velocity and attenuation, pre-stack joint inversion can provide a more detailed image of the subsurface. This technique is particularly useful in imaging both the near-surface and deep subsurface, as well as in imaging both homogeneous and heterogeneous subsurface structures.

However, there are also limitations to pre-stack joint inversion. One of the main challenges is the presence of noise and interference in the seismic data. This can make it difficult to accurately interpret the data and create a detailed image of the subsurface. Additionally, pre-stack joint inversion relies on assumptions about the properties of the subsurface, such as homogeneity and isotropy, which may not always be accurate.

### Subsection: 15.1c.2 Post-Stack Joint Inversion

Post-stack joint inversion is another type of joint inversion that is commonly used in geophysics. It involves the use of post-stack data, which is data collected after the seismic event has occurred. This type of inversion is particularly useful in complex geological environments where traditional pre-stack inversion may not be sufficient.

The process of post-stack joint inversion involves the use of both P-waves and S-waves to image the subsurface. By analyzing the properties of these waves, such as their velocity and attenuation, post-stack joint inversion can provide a more detailed image of the subsurface. This technique is particularly useful in imaging both the near-surface and deep subsurface, as well as in imaging both homogeneous and heterogeneous subsurface structures.

However, there are also limitations to post-stack joint inversion. One of the main challenges is the presence of noise and interference in the seismic data. This can make it difficult to accurately interpret the data and create a detailed image of the subsurface. Additionally, post-stack joint inversion relies on assumptions about the properties of the subsurface, such as homogeneity and isotropy, which may not always be accurate.


### Conclusion
In this chapter, we have explored the concept of seismic inversion and its applications in near-surface geophysical imaging. We have learned that seismic inversion is a powerful tool for interpreting seismic data and creating detailed images of the subsurface. By using seismic inversion, we can extract valuable information about the subsurface, such as the presence of geological structures, changes in rock properties, and the location of potential hazards.

We have also discussed the different types of seismic inversion, including deterministic and stochastic inversion, and their respective advantages and limitations. We have seen how deterministic inversion is based on a mathematical model and can provide accurate results, but it may not always be applicable in complex geological environments. On the other hand, stochastic inversion is more flexible and can handle a wider range of geological conditions, but it may also introduce more uncertainty in the results.

Furthermore, we have explored the various techniques used in seismic inversion, such as migration, deblurring, and velocity analysis. These techniques are essential for improving the quality of seismic data and obtaining more accurate inversion results. We have also discussed the importance of understanding the underlying geology and the limitations of seismic data in the inversion process.

In conclusion, seismic inversion is a valuable tool for near-surface geophysical imaging, and it has numerous applications in various fields, such as oil and gas exploration, groundwater studies, and hazard assessment. By understanding the principles and techniques of seismic inversion, we can effectively interpret seismic data and gain valuable insights into the subsurface.

The main goal of seismic migration is to transform the recorded seismic data into a more accurate representation of the subsurface structure. This is achieved by correcting for the effects of wave propagation, which can distort the original signal and make it difficult to interpret. By applying migration techniques, we can improve the resolution and quality of the seismic images, allowing us to better understand the subsurface structure and its properties.

## Chapter: - Chapter 16: Seismic Forward Modeling:




### Section: 16.1 Wave Propagation Modeling:

In this section, we will explore the concept of wave propagation modeling, which is a crucial aspect of seismic forward modeling. Wave propagation refers to the movement of seismic waves through the Earth's subsurface. Understanding how these waves propagate is essential for accurately predicting the behavior of seismic signals and for interpreting the results of seismic surveys.

#### 16.1a Finite Difference Method

The Finite Difference Method (FDM) is a numerical technique used to approximate solutions of partial differential equations (PDEs). In the context of seismic forward modeling, FDM is used to model the propagation of seismic waves through the Earth's subsurface.

The FDM is based on the Taylor series expansion, which allows us to approximate the derivatives of a function at a given point by using the values of the function at nearby points. In the context of seismic forward modeling, the FDM is used to approximate the propagation of seismic waves through the subsurface by discretizing the subsurface into a grid of points and approximating the derivatives of the wavefield at each point.

The FDM is particularly useful for modeling wave propagation in complex subsurface structures, such as layered media or heterogeneous media. By discretizing the subsurface into a grid of points, the FDM allows us to model the propagation of seismic waves through these complex structures in a systematic and efficient manner.

The FDM is also well-suited for modeling wave propagation in anisotropic media, where the properties of the medium (such as the velocity of seismic waves) vary in different directions. By discretizing the subsurface into a grid of points, the FDM allows us to model the anisotropic properties of the medium in a straightforward manner.

However, the FDM also has its limitations. For example, it is a first-order numerical method, which means that the accuracy of the approximation depends on the size of the grid points. Smaller grid points result in a more accurate approximation, but also require more computational resources.

In the next section, we will explore another numerical method for modeling wave propagation: the Finite Element Method (FEM).

#### 16.1b Finite Element Method

The Finite Element Method (FEM) is another numerical technique used to approximate solutions of partial differential equations (PDEs). In the context of seismic forward modeling, FEM is used to model the propagation of seismic waves through the Earth's subsurface.

The FEM is based on the concept of dividing a continuous domain into a finite number of smaller, simpler domains called finite elements. These elements are connected at points called nodes, forming a mesh. The behavior of the system is then approximated by interpolating the solution within each element from the known values at the nodes.

In the context of seismic forward modeling, the FEM is used to model the propagation of seismic waves through the subsurface by discretizing the subsurface into a mesh of finite elements. The behavior of the wavefield is then approximated by interpolating the wavefield within each element from the known values at the nodes.

The FEM is particularly useful for modeling wave propagation in complex subsurface structures, such as layered media or heterogeneous media. By discretizing the subsurface into a mesh of finite elements, the FEM allows us to model the propagation of seismic waves through these complex structures in a systematic and efficient manner.

The FEM is also well-suited for modeling wave propagation in anisotropic media, where the properties of the medium (such as the velocity of seismic waves) vary in different directions. By discretizing the subsurface into a mesh of finite elements, the FEM allows us to model the anisotropic properties of the medium in a straightforward manner.

However, the FEM also has its limitations. For example, it is a second-order numerical method, which means that the accuracy of the approximation depends on the size of the elements. Smaller elements result in a more accurate approximation, but also require more computational resources.

In the next section, we will explore the concept of wave propagation modeling in more detail, focusing on the application of the Finite Difference Method and the Finite Element Method in seismic forward modeling.

#### 16.1c Ray Tracing Method

The Ray Tracing Method is a numerical technique used to model the propagation of seismic waves through the Earth's subsurface. This method is based on the principle of ray optics, where seismic waves are represented as rays that travel through the subsurface.

In the context of seismic forward modeling, the Ray Tracing Method is used to model the propagation of seismic waves through the subsurface by tracing the path of these rays. The subsurface is represented as a series of layers, each with its own velocity and density. The rays are then traced through these layers, taking into account the changes in velocity and density.

The Ray Tracing Method is particularly useful for modeling wave propagation in layered media, where the subsurface can be represented as a series of layers with different properties. By tracing the rays through these layers, the Ray Tracing Method allows us to model the propagation of seismic waves through these complex structures in a systematic and efficient manner.

The Ray Tracing Method is also well-suited for modeling wave propagation in anisotropic media, where the properties of the medium (such as the velocity of seismic waves) vary in different directions. By tracing the rays through these layers, the Ray Tracing Method allows us to model the anisotropic properties of the medium in a straightforward manner.

However, the Ray Tracing Method also has its limitations. For example, it is a first-order numerical method, which means that the accuracy of the approximation depends on the size of the grid points. Smaller grid points result in a more accurate approximation, but also require more computational resources.

In the next section, we will explore the concept of wave propagation modeling in more detail, focusing on the application of the Ray Tracing Method in seismic forward modeling.

#### 16.1d Wave Equation Method

The Wave Equation Method is a numerical technique used to model the propagation of seismic waves through the Earth's subsurface. This method is based on the wave equation, a second-order linear partial differential equation that describes the propagation of a variety of waves, such as sound waves, light waves, and seismic waves.

In the context of seismic forward modeling, the Wave Equation Method is used to model the propagation of seismic waves through the subsurface by solving the wave equation. The subsurface is represented as a continuous medium with a specific velocity and density. The wave equation is then solved for the displacement of the medium at each point in space and time.

The Wave Equation Method is particularly useful for modeling wave propagation in continuous media, where the subsurface cannot be easily represented as a series of layers. By solving the wave equation, the Wave Equation Method allows us to model the propagation of seismic waves through these complex structures in a systematic and efficient manner.

The Wave Equation Method is also well-suited for modeling wave propagation in isotropic media, where the properties of the medium (such as the velocity of seismic waves) are the same in all directions. By solving the wave equation, the Wave Equation Method allows us to model the isotropic properties of the medium in a straightforward manner.

However, the Wave Equation Method also has its limitations. For example, it is a second-order numerical method, which means that the accuracy of the approximation depends on the size of the grid points. Smaller grid points result in a more accurate approximation, but also require more computational resources.

In the next section, we will explore the concept of wave propagation modeling in more detail, focusing on the application of the Wave Equation Method in seismic forward modeling.

#### 16.1e Seismic Wave Propagation in Complex Media

Seismic wave propagation in complex media is a challenging problem due to the inherent complexity of the Earth's subsurface. The subsurface is often characterized by heterogeneity, anisotropy, and non-uniformity, which can significantly affect the propagation of seismic waves. In this section, we will discuss some of the techniques used to model seismic wave propagation in complex media.

##### 16.1e.1 Multiscale Modeling

Multiscale modeling is a technique used to model the propagation of seismic waves in complex media. This technique is based on the concept of representing the subsurface as a collection of smaller, simpler models or units, which are then combined to form a larger model of the subsurface.

The multiscale modeling approach allows us to capture the complexity of the subsurface while maintaining computational efficiency. By representing the subsurface as a collection of smaller models, we can avoid the need to solve the wave equation for the entire subsurface, which can be computationally intensive. Instead, we can solve the wave equation for each individual model and then combine the results to obtain the overall solution.

##### 16.1e.2 Stochastic Modeling

Stochastic modeling is another technique used to model the propagation of seismic waves in complex media. This technique is based on the concept of representing the subsurface as a random field, where the properties of the medium (such as the velocity and density) are described by a probability distribution.

The stochastic modeling approach allows us to account for the uncertainty and variability in the properties of the subsurface. By representing the subsurface as a random field, we can account for the variability in the properties of the medium, which can significantly affect the propagation of seismic waves.

##### 16.1e.3 Hybrid Modeling

Hybrid modeling is a combination of the multiscale and stochastic modeling approaches. In this approach, the subsurface is represented as a collection of smaller models, and each model is characterized by a probability distribution.

The hybrid modeling approach allows us to capture the complexity of the subsurface while accounting for the uncertainty and variability in the properties of the subsurface. By combining the multiscale and stochastic modeling approaches, we can obtain a more accurate and robust model of the subsurface.

In the next section, we will discuss some of the applications of these techniques in seismic forward modeling.

### Conclusion

We have explored the fundamental principles that govern the propagation of seismic waves through the Earth's subsurface, and how these principles can be applied to create accurate models of the subsurface. 

We have also discussed the various techniques and algorithms used in seismic forward modeling, including the finite difference method, the finite element method, and the ray tracing method. Each of these methods has its strengths and weaknesses, and the choice of method depends on the specific requirements of the project.

Finally, we have highlighted the importance of understanding the limitations and uncertainties associated with seismic forward modeling. Despite its power and utility, seismic forward modeling is not a perfect tool, and it is crucial to be aware of its limitations when interpreting the results.

In conclusion, seismic forward modeling is a powerful tool for near-surface geophysical imaging, but it requires a deep understanding of the underlying principles and techniques. With this knowledge, we can create accurate and reliable models of the Earth's subsurface, which are essential for a wide range of applications, from mineral exploration to environmental studies.

## Chapter: Chapter 17: Seismic Inverse Modeling

### Introduction

In the realm of geophysics, the ability to accurately interpret and understand the subsurface properties of the Earth is of paramount importance. This is where the concept of seismic inverse modeling comes into play. Chapter 17 of our "Near-Surface Geophysical Imaging Textbook" delves into the intricacies of seismic inverse modeling, a powerful tool that allows us to infer the properties of the subsurface from seismic data.

Seismic inverse modeling is a complex process that involves the use of mathematical models and algorithms to interpret seismic data. It is a crucial step in the process of geophysical imaging, as it allows us to create a detailed picture of the subsurface structure and properties. This chapter will provide a comprehensive overview of the principles and techniques involved in seismic inverse modeling.

We will begin by exploring the fundamental concepts of seismic inverse modeling, including the basic principles of wave propagation and the role of seismic data in the process. We will then delve into the mathematical models and algorithms used in seismic inverse modeling, providing a detailed explanation of how these tools are used to interpret seismic data.

Throughout the chapter, we will also discuss the limitations and challenges of seismic inverse modeling, as well as the ongoing research and advancements in the field. By the end of this chapter, readers should have a solid understanding of the principles and techniques involved in seismic inverse modeling, and be able to apply this knowledge in their own geophysical imaging projects.

In the world of geophysics, seismic inverse modeling is a powerful tool that allows us to unlock the secrets of the subsurface. This chapter aims to provide a comprehensive guide to this fascinating field, equipping readers with the knowledge and skills they need to navigate the complex world of near-surface geophysical imaging.




### Introduction to Spectral Element Method

The Spectral Element Method (SEM) is a numerical technique used in the field of geophysics for solving partial differential equations (PDEs). It is a high-order finite element method that uses spectral interpolation to approximate the solution of PDEs. The SEM was first introduced by A. T. Patera in 1984 and has since been widely used in various applications, including seismic forward modeling.

The SEM is particularly useful for solving PDEs that involve high-order derivatives, such as the wave equation in seismic forward modeling. The method is based on the idea of representing the solution as a sum of basis functions, which are typically high-order polynomials. These basis functions are chosen such that they satisfy certain interpolation conditions, leading to a high-order approximation of the solution.

The SEM is implemented in a number of freely available codes, including the Spectral Element Equations Solver (SEES) and the Spectral Element Toolkit (SET). These codes provide a flexible and efficient implementation of the SEM, allowing for the solution of a wide range of PDEs.

#### 16.1c.1 Implementation of the Spectral Element Method

The implementation of the SEM involves the following steps:

1. Discretization of the domain into a set of spectral elements.
2. Definition of the basis functions for each element.
3. Assembly of the global stiffness matrix and load vector.
4. Solution of the resulting linear system.
5. Post-processing of the solution.

The first step involves discretizing the domain into a set of spectral elements. This is typically done using a structured grid, where each element is a simple geometric shape, such as a triangle or a quadrilateral. The number of elements and their size can be adjusted to control the level of detail in the solution.

The next step is to define the basis functions for each element. These are typically high-order polynomials, such as Legendre or Chebyshev polynomials, and are chosen such that they satisfy certain interpolation conditions. The choice of basis functions depends on the specific problem and can significantly affect the accuracy and efficiency of the solution.

The global stiffness matrix and load vector are then assembled by summing the contributions from each element. This involves the evaluation of the basis functions and their derivatives at the element interfaces, as well as the assembly of the element matrices and vectors.

The resulting linear system is then solved using a suitable solver. This can be done using direct methods, such as Gaussian elimination or LU decomposition, or iterative methods, such as the conjugate gradient method.

Finally, the solution is post-processed to extract the desired information, such as the displacement or stress at specific points in the domain. This can be done using various techniques, such as interpolation or differentiation of the solution.

#### 16.1c.2 Advantages and Limitations of the Spectral Element Method

The SEM offers several advantages over other numerical methods, including:

1. High-order accuracy: The SEM provides a high-order approximation of the solution, leading to more accurate results.
2. Stability: The SEM is a stable method, meaning that small errors in the input data do not lead to large errors in the solution.
3. Efficiency: The SEM is an efficient method, particularly for problems with high-order derivatives.

However, the SEM also has some limitations, including:

1. Complexity: The implementation of the SEM can be complex, particularly for problems with complex geometries or boundary conditions.
2. Memory requirements: The SEM requires a significant amount of memory to store the global stiffness matrix and load vector, which can be a limitation for large-scale problems.
3. Sensitivity to the choice of basis functions: The accuracy of the SEM can be significantly affected by the choice of basis functions, which can be challenging to determine for complex problems.

Despite these limitations, the SEM remains a powerful tool for solving PDEs in geophysics and other fields. Its high-order accuracy and efficiency make it particularly well-suited for problems involving high-order derivatives, such as seismic forward modeling.




#### 16.1d Boundary Element Method

The Boundary Element Method (BEM) is a numerical technique used in geophysics for solving partial differential equations (PDEs). It is a boundary-based method that uses the boundary conditions of the problem to solve for the solution. The BEM is particularly useful for solving PDEs that involve high-order derivatives, such as the wave equation in seismic forward modeling.

The BEM is implemented in a number of freely available codes, including the Boundary Element Equations Solver (BEES) and the Boundary Element Toolkit (BET). These codes provide a flexible and efficient implementation of the BEM, allowing for the solution of a wide range of PDEs.

#### 16.1d.1 Implementation of the Boundary Element Method

The implementation of the BEM involves the following steps:

1. Discretization of the boundary into a set of boundary elements.
2. Definition of the boundary conditions for each element.
3. Assembly of the global boundary element matrix and load vector.
4. Solution of the resulting linear system.
5. Post-processing of the solution.

The first step involves discretizing the boundary into a set of boundary elements. This is typically done using a structured grid, where each element is a simple geometric shape, such as a triangle or a quadrilateral. The number of elements and their size can be adjusted to control the level of detail in the solution.

The next step is to define the boundary conditions for each element. These are typically given in terms of the normal and tangential derivatives of the solution at the boundary. These boundary conditions are used to construct the boundary element matrix and load vector.

The assembly of the global boundary element matrix and load vector involves summing the contributions from each element. This is typically done using numerical integration techniques, such as the Gauss quadrature.

The solution of the resulting linear system is typically done using a direct solver, such as the LU decomposition. The solution provides the values of the solution at the boundary.

The post-processing of the solution involves calculating the solution at any desired point in the interior of the solution domain. This is typically done using the boundary element method, which attempts to use the given boundary conditions to fit boundary values into the integral equation.

The BEM is applicable to problems for which Green's functions can be calculated. These usually involve fields in linear homogeneous media. This places considerable restrictions on the range and generality of problems to which boundary elements can usefully be applied. Nonlinearities can be included in the formulation, although they will generally introduce volume integrals which then require the volume to be discretised before solution can be attempted, removing one of the most often cited advantages of BEM. A useful technique for treating the volume integral without discretising the volume is the dual-reciprocity method. The technique approximates part of the integrand using radial basis functions (local interpolating functions) and converts the volume integral into boundary integral after collocating at selected points distributed throughout the volume domain (including the boundary). In the dual-reciprocity BEM, although there is no need to discretize the volume into meshes, unknowns at chosen points inside the solution domain are involved.

# Title: Near-Surface Geophysical Imaging Textbook":

## Chapter: - Chapter 17: Seismic Migration:




### Section: 17.1 Pre-stack Migration:

Pre-stack migration is a crucial step in the seismic imaging process. It involves the migration of seismic data from the subsurface to the surface, allowing for a more accurate interpretation of the data. In this section, we will discuss the basics of pre-stack migration, including its purpose and the different types of pre-stack migration methods.

#### 17.1a Kirchhoff Migration

Kirchhoff migration is a type of pre-stack migration method that is commonly used in the oil and gas industry. It is based on the Kirchhoff's integral theorem, which states that the displacement of a point on a surface can be calculated by integrating the normal derivative of the surface over the entire surface. This theorem is used to calculate the displacement of a point on the subsurface from the surface data.

The Kirchhoff migration method involves the use of a migration operator, which is a mathematical function that maps the subsurface data to the surface data. This operator is based on the Kirchhoff's integral theorem and is used to calculate the displacement of a point on the subsurface from the surface data. The migration operator is typically represented by the symbol M and is defined as:

$$
M = \int_{\Omega} N_{11}\frac{\partial\delta v_1}{\partial x_1} + N_{12}\frac{\partial\delta v_1}{\partial x_2}\,d\Omega = -\int_{\Omega} p_1 \delta v_1 \,d\Omega \\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{22}\frac{\partial\delta v_2}{\partial x_2} + N_{12}\frac{\partial\delta v_2}{\partial x_1}\,d\Omega = -\int_{\Omega} p_2 \delta v_2 \,d\Omega \\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{11}\frac{\partial w}{\partial x_1}\frac{\partial\delta w}{\partial x_1} - M_{11}\frac{\partial^2 \delta w}{\partial^2 x_1} \,d\Omega\\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{22}\frac{\partial w}{\partial x_2}\frac{\partial\delta w}{\partial x_2} - M_{22}\frac{\partial^2 \delta w}{\partial^2 x_2} \,d\Omega\\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{12}\left(\frac{\partial \delta w}{\partial x_1}\frac{\partial\delta w}{\partial x_2} + \frac{\partial w}{\partial x_1}\frac{\partial\delta w}{\partial x_2}\right) - 2M_{12}\frac{\partial^2 \delta w}{\partial x_1\partial x_2} \,d\Omega = -\int_{\Omega} p_3 \delta w \,d\Omega\\

The resulting governing equations are

$$
\begin{align}
\frac{\partial^2 w}{\partial x_1^2} - M_{11}\frac{\partial^2 w}{\partial^2 x_1} &= p_1 \\
\frac{\partial^2 w}{\partial x_2^2} - M_{22}\frac{\partial^2 w}{\partial^2 x_2} &= p_2 \\
\frac{\partial^2 w}{\partial x_1\partial x_2} - 2M_{12}\frac{\partial^2 w}{\partial x_1\partial x_2} &= p_3
\end{align}
$$

These equations can be solved using various numerical methods to obtain the migration operator M. Once the migration operator is known, the displacement of a point on the subsurface can be calculated by applying the operator to the surface data.

Kirchhoff migration is a powerful tool for imaging the subsurface, but it has its limitations. It is most effective when the subsurface is relatively flat and homogeneous. In more complex geological structures, other migration methods may be more suitable. In the next section, we will discuss another type of pre-stack migration method known as finite-difference migration.





#### 17.1b Wave-equation Migration

Wave-equation migration is another type of pre-stack migration method that is commonly used in the oil and gas industry. It is based on the wave equation, which describes the propagation of seismic waves through a medium. The wave equation is given by:

$$
\frac{\partial^2 u}{\partial t^2} = c^2 \frac{\partial^2 u}{\partial x^2}
$$

where $u$ is the displacement of the wave, $t$ is time, $x$ is the spatial coordinate, and $c$ is the wave speed.

The wave-equation migration method involves the use of a migration operator, which is a mathematical function that maps the subsurface data to the surface data. This operator is based on the wave equation and is used to calculate the displacement of a point on the subsurface from the surface data. The migration operator is typically represented by the symbol M and is defined as:

$$
M = \int_{\Omega} N_{11}\frac{\partial\delta v_1}{\partial x_1} + N_{12}\frac{\partial\delta v_1}{\partial x_2}\,d\Omega = -\int_{\Omega} p_1 \delta v_1 \,d\Omega \\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{22}\frac{\partial\delta v_2}{\partial x_2} + N_{12}\frac{\partial\delta v_2}{\partial x_1}\,d\Omega = -\int_{\Omega} p_2 \delta v_2 \,d\Omega \\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{11}\frac{\partial w}{\partial x_1}\frac{\partial\delta w}{\partial x_1} - M_{11}\frac{\partial^2 \delta w}{\partial^2 x_1} \,d\Omega\\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{22}\frac{\partial w}{\partial x_2}\frac{\partial\delta w}{\partial x_2} - M_{22}\frac{\partial^2 \delta w}{\partial^2 x_2} \,d\Omega\\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{12}\left(\frac{\partial \delta w}{\partial x_1}\frac{\partial\delta w}{\partial x_2} + \frac{\partial w}{\partial x_1}\frac{\partial\delta w}{\partial x_2}\right) - M_{12}\frac{\partial^2 \delta w}{\partial x_1\partial x_2} \,d\Omega\\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{11}\frac{\partial w}{\partial x_1}\frac{\partial^2 \delta w}{\partial x_1\partial t} - M_{11}\frac{\partial^2 \delta w}{\partial x_1\partial t} \,d\Omega\\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{22}\frac{\partial w}{\partial x_2}\frac{\partial^2 \delta w}{\partial x_2\partial t} - M_{22}\frac{\partial^2 \delta w}{\partial x_2\partial t} \,d\Omega\\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{12}\left(\frac{\partial w}{\partial x_1}\frac{\partial^2 \delta w}{\partial x_2\partial t} + \frac{\partial w}{\partial x_2}\frac{\partial^2 \delta w}{\partial x_1\partial t}\right) - M_{12}\frac{\partial^2 \delta w}{\partial x_1\partial x_2\partial t} \,d\Omega\\
$$

where $N_{ij}$ are the shape functions, $p_i$ are the pressure coefficients, and $w$ is the displacement function.

The wave-equation migration method is particularly useful for imaging complex subsurface structures, such as faults and interfaces, as it takes into account the wave propagation effects. However, it requires a good understanding of the wave equation and the subsurface properties in order to accurately interpret the migration results.

#### 17.1c Common Applications

Seismic migration, both pre-stack and post-stack, has a wide range of applications in the field of geophysics. These applications span across various industries, including oil and gas exploration, mineral exploration, and environmental studies. In this section, we will discuss some of the common applications of seismic migration.

##### Oil and Gas Exploration

Seismic migration is a crucial tool in the oil and gas industry. It is used to image the subsurface structures and properties, which are essential for understanding the distribution of hydrocarbons. The Kirchhoff migration and wave-equation migration methods, as discussed in the previous sections, are commonly used in this industry. These methods allow for the accurate imaging of complex subsurface structures, such as faults and interfaces, which are crucial for identifying potential hydrocarbon reservoirs.

##### Mineral Exploration

Seismic migration is also used in mineral exploration, particularly in the search for precious metals and minerals. The imaging of subsurface structures can provide valuable information about the distribution of these resources. For example, the wave-equation migration method can be used to image the propagation of seismic waves through the subsurface, which can provide insights into the mineral content of the rocks.

##### Environmental Studies

In the field of environmental studies, seismic migration is used to image the subsurface structures and properties for various purposes. For instance, it can be used to study the effects of groundwater extraction on the subsurface structures, or to image the subsurface for the detection of underground waste disposal sites. The Kirchhoff migration and wave-equation migration methods are particularly useful in these applications, as they allow for the accurate imaging of the subsurface structures and properties.

In conclusion, seismic migration is a powerful tool in the field of geophysics, with a wide range of applications. Its ability to accurately image the subsurface structures and properties makes it an essential tool in various industries, including oil and gas exploration, mineral exploration, and environmental studies.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the complex world of seismic migration, a critical aspect of near-surface geophysical imaging. We have explored the fundamental principles that govern the migration process, and how these principles are applied in the imaging of the near-surface geology. 

We have also discussed the various methods of seismic migration, including the pre-stack and post-stack methods, and their respective advantages and disadvantages. The chapter has also highlighted the importance of understanding the subsurface properties and the effects of migration on these properties. 

In conclusion, seismic migration is a powerful tool in the exploration and understanding of the near-surface geology. It provides a detailed image of the subsurface, which can be used for various purposes, including the identification of potential hydrocarbon reserves, the assessment of groundwater resources, and the prediction of seismic hazards. However, it is a complex process that requires a deep understanding of the underlying principles and the careful application of the appropriate methods.

## Chapter: Chapter 18: Post-stack Migration

### Introduction

In the realm of geophysics, the process of migration is a crucial step in the interpretation of seismic data. It is a mathematical technique used to enhance the quality of seismic images by reducing noise and artifacts. In this chapter, we will delve into the post-stack migration, a specific type of migration technique that is widely used in the industry.

Post-stack migration is a process that is applied to the migrated stacked data. It is a powerful tool that can provide a more accurate image of the subsurface structures. The process involves the application of a migration operator to the stacked data, which is a result of the pre-stack migration. This operator is designed to correct the effects of the medium and the source, and to focus the energy on the subsurface structures.

The chapter will provide a comprehensive overview of the post-stack migration, starting from its basic principles to its practical applications. We will discuss the mathematical foundations of the post-stack migration, including the migration operator and the migration equation. We will also explore the different types of post-stack migration methods, such as the Kirchhoff migration and the wave-equation migration.

Furthermore, we will discuss the advantages and limitations of the post-stack migration. We will also touch upon the challenges and the solutions associated with the post-stack migration. The chapter will also provide examples and case studies to illustrate the practical applications of the post-stack migration.

In conclusion, this chapter aims to provide a solid foundation for understanding the post-stack migration. It is designed to equip the readers with the necessary knowledge and skills to apply the post-stack migration in their own work. Whether you are a student, a researcher, or a professional in the field of geophysics, this chapter will serve as a valuable resource for you.




#### 17.1c Reverse Time Migration

Reverse time migration (RTM) is a powerful pre-stack migration method that is used to image the subsurface from surface data. It is based on the principle of reverse time, where the migration process is performed in reverse order, starting from the surface data and moving towards the subsurface. This method is particularly useful for imaging complex structures and interfaces, such as faults and unconformities, which are often difficult to image using traditional migration methods.

The RTM method involves the use of a migration operator, which is a mathematical function that maps the surface data to the subsurface data. This operator is based on the wave equation and is used to calculate the displacement of a point on the subsurface from the surface data. The migration operator is typically represented by the symbol M and is defined as:

$$
M = \int_{\Omega} N_{11}\frac{\partial\delta v_1}{\partial x_1} + N_{12}\frac{\partial\delta v_1}{\partial x_2}\,d\Omega = -\int_{\Omega} p_1 \delta v_1 \,d\Omega \\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{22}\frac{\partial\delta v_2}{\partial x_2} + N_{12}\frac{\partial\delta v_2}{\partial x_1}\,d\Omega = -\int_{\Omega} p_2 \delta v_2 \,d\Omega \\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{11}\frac{\partial w}{\partial x_1}\frac{\partial\delta w}{\partial x_1} - M_{11}\frac{\partial^2 \delta w}{\partial^2 x_1} \,d\Omega\\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{22}\frac{\partial w}{\partial x_2}\frac{\partial\delta w}{\partial x_2} - M_{22}\frac{\partial^2 \delta w}{\partial^2 x_2} \,d\Omega\\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{12}\left(\frac{\partial \delta w}{\partial x_1}\frac{\partial\delta w}{\partial x_2} + \frac{\partial w}{\partial x_1}\frac{\partial\delta w}{\partial x_2}\right) - M_{12}\frac{\partial^2 \delta w}{\partial x_1\partial x_2} \,d\Omega\\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{11}\frac{\partial w}{\partial x_1}\frac{\partial^2 \delta w}{\partial x_1\partial t} - M_{11}\frac{\partial^2 \delta w}{\partial x_1\partial t} \,d\Omega\\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{22}\frac{\partial w}{\partial x_2}\frac{\partial^2 \delta w}{\partial x_2\partial t} - M_{22}\frac{\partial^2 \delta w}{\partial x_2\partial t} \,d\Omega\\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{12}\frac{\partial w}{\partial x_1}\frac{\partial^2 \delta w}{\partial x_2\partial t} - M_{12}\frac{\partial^2 \delta w}{\partial x_1\partial x_2\partial t} \,d\Omega\\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{11}\frac{\partial w}{\partial x_1}\frac{\partial^3 \delta w}{\partial x_1^2\partial t} - M_{11}\frac{\partial^3 \delta w}{\partial x_1^2\partial t} \,d\Omega\\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{22}\frac{\partial w}{\partial x_2}\frac{\partial^3 \delta w}{\partial x_2^2\partial t} - M_{22}\frac{\partial^3 \delta w}{\partial x_2^2\partial t} \,d\Omega\\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{12}\frac{\partial w}{\partial x_1}\frac{\partial^3 \delta w}{\partial x_1\partial x_2\partial t} - M_{12}\frac{\partial^3 \delta w}{\partial x_1\partial x_2\partial t} \,d\Omega\\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{11}\frac{\partial w}{\partial x_1}\frac{\partial^4 \delta w}{\partial x_1^3\partial t} - M_{11}\frac{\partial^4 \delta w}{\partial x_1^3\partial t} \,d\Omega\\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{22}\frac{\partial w}{\partial x_2}\frac{\partial^4 \delta w}{\partial x_2^3\partial t} - M_{22}\frac{\partial^4 \delta w}{\partial x_2^3\partial t} \,d\Omega\\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{12}\frac{\partial w}{\partial x_1}\frac{\partial^4 \delta w}{\partial x_1^2\partial x_2\partial t} - M_{12}\frac{\partial^4 \delta w}{\partial x_1^2\partial x_2\partial t} \,d\Omega\\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{11}\frac{\partial w}{\partial x_1}\frac{\partial^5 \delta w}{\partial x_1^4\partial t} - M_{11}\frac{\partial^5 \delta w}{\partial x_1^4\partial t} \,d\Omega\\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{22}\frac{\partial w}{\partial x_2}\frac{\partial^5 \delta w}{\partial x_2^4\partial t} - M_{22}\frac{\partial^5 \delta w}{\partial x_2^4\partial t} \,d\Omega\\

&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{11}\frac{\partial w}{\partial x_1}\frac{\partial^6 \delta w}{\partial x_1^5\partial t} - M_{11}\frac{\partial^6 \delta w}{\partial x_1^5\partial t} \,d\Omega\\

&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{11}\frac{\partial w}{\partial x_1}\frac{\partial^{30} \delta w}{\partial x_1^{29}\partial t} - M_{11}\frac{\partial^{30} \delta w}{\partial x_1^{29}\partial t} \,d\Omega\\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{22}\frac{\partial w}{\partial x_2}\frac{\partial^{30} \delta w}{\partial x_2^{29}\partial t} - M_{22}\frac{\partial^{30} \delta w}{\partial x_2^{29}\partial t} \,d\Omega\\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{12}\frac{\partial w}{\partial x_1}\frac{\partial^{30} \delta w}{\partial x_1^{28}\partial x_2\partial t} - M_{12}\frac{\partial^{30} \delta w}{\partial x_1^{28}\partial x_2\partial t} \,d\Omega\\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{11}\frac{\partial w}{\partial x_1}\frac{\partial^{31} \delta w}{\partial x_1^{30}\partial t} - M_{11}\frac{\partial^{31} \delta w}{\partial x_1^{30}\partial t} \,d\Omega\\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{22}\frac{\partial w}{\partial x_2}\frac{\partial^{31} \delta w}{\partial x_2^{30}\partial t} - M_{22}\frac{\partial^{31} \delta w}{\partial x_2^{30}\partial t} \,d\Omega\\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{12}\frac{\partial w}{\partial x_1}\frac{\partial^{31} \delta w}{\partial x_1^{29}\partial x_2\partial t} - M_{12}\frac{\partial^{31} \delta w}{\partial x_1^{29}\partial x_2\partial t} \,d\Omega\\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{11}\frac{\partial w}{\partial x_1}\frac{\partial^{32} \delta w}{\partial x_1^{31}\partial t} - M_{11}\frac{\partial^{32} \delta w}{\partial x_1^{31}\partial t} \,d\Omega\\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{22}\frac{\partial w}{\partial x_2}\frac{\partial^{32} \delta w}{\partial x_2^{31}\partial t} - M_{22}\frac{\partial^{32} \delta w}{\partial x_2^{31}\partial t} \,d\Omega\\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{12}\frac{\partial w}{\partial x_1}\frac{\partial^{32} \delta w}{\partial x_1^{30}\partial x_2\partial t} - M_{12}\frac{\partial^{32} \delta w}{\partial x_1^{30}\partial x_2\partial t} \,d\Omega\\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{11}\frac{\partial w}{\partial x_1}\frac{\partial^{33} \delta w}{\partial x_1^{32}\partial t} - M_{11}\frac{\partial^{33} \delta w}{\partial x_1^{32}\partial t} \,d\Omega\\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{22}\frac{\partial w}{\partial x_2}\frac{\partial^{33} \delta w}{\partial x_2^{32}\partial t} - M_{22}\frac{\partial^{33} \delta w}{\partial x_2^{32}\partial t} \,d\Omega\\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{12}\frac{\partial w}{\partial x_1}\frac{\partial^{33} \delta w}{\partial x_1^{31}\partial x_2\partial t} - M_{12}\frac{\partial^{33} \delta w}{\partial x_1^{31}\partial x_2\partial t} \,d\Omega\\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{11}\


#### 17.1d Least-squares Migration

Least-squares migration (LSM) is another powerful pre-stack migration method that is used to image the subsurface from surface data. It is based on the principle of least squares, where the migration process is performed by minimizing the sum of the squares of the differences between the observed data and the predicted data. This method is particularly useful for imaging complex structures and interfaces, such as faults and unconformities, which are often difficult to image using traditional migration methods.

The LSM method involves the use of a migration operator, which is a mathematical function that maps the surface data to the subsurface data. This operator is based on the wave equation and is used to calculate the displacement of a point on the subsurface from the surface data. The migration operator is typically represented by the symbol M and is defined as:

$$
M = \int_{\Omega} N_{11}\frac{\partial\delta v_1}{\partial x_1} + N_{12}\frac{\partial\delta v_1}{\partial x_2}\,d\Omega = -\int_{\Omega} p_1 \delta v_1 \,d\Omega \\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{22}\frac{\partial\delta v_2}{\partial x_2} + N_{12}\frac{\partial\delta v_2}{\partial x_1}\,d\Omega = -\int_{\Omega} p_2 \delta v_2 \,d\Omega \\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{11}\frac{\partial w}{\partial x_1}\frac{\partial\delta w}{\partial x_1} - M_{11}\frac{\partial^2 \delta w}{\partial^2 x_1} \,d\Omega\\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{22}\frac{\partial w}{\partial x_2}\frac{\partial\delta w}{\partial x_2} - M_{22}\frac{\partial^2 \delta w}{\partial^2 x_2} \,d\Omega\\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{12}\left(\frac{\partial \delta w}{\partial x_1}\frac{\partial\delta w}{\partial x_2} + \frac{\partial w}{\partial x_1}\frac{\partial\delta w}{\partial x_2}\right) - M_{12}\frac{\partial^2 \delta w}{\partial x_1\partial x_2} \,d\Omega\\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{11}\frac{\partial w}{\partial x_1}\frac{\partial^2 \delta w}{\partial x_1\partial t} - M_{11}\frac{\partial^2 \delta w}{\partial x_1\partial t} \,d\Omega\\
&+ \int_{\Omega} N_{22}\frac{\partial 
$$

The LSM method then minimizes the sum of the squares of the differences between the observed data and the predicted data, which is represented by the term $\int_{\Omega} (p - M\delta w)^2 \,d\Omega$. This minimization process results in a migration operator that maps the surface data to the subsurface data in a way that minimizes the differences between the observed and predicted data.

The LSM method has been applied to a wide range of problems since it was first published in 1993. It has been used in the optimization of glass recycling, in the development of new algorithms and workflows for geodetic data processing, and in the imaging of complex structures and interfaces in the subsurface. Its ability to handle complex structures and interfaces makes it a valuable tool for geophysical imaging.

It allows us to obtain a more detailed and accurate understanding of the Earth's subsurface, which is crucial in various fields such as oil and gas exploration, groundwater studies, and earthquake research. As technology continues to advance, we can expect seismic migration to play an even more significant role in our understanding of the Earth's subsurface.

## Chapter 18: Seismic Interpretation
